title="Employment-to-population ratio">Employment-to-population ratio</a></td>\n<td>68.0% <span style="font-size:85%;"> (2017)</span></td>\n<td style="text-align: right;"><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-&#3626;&#3633;&#3591;&#3588;&#3617;_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-&#3626;&#3633;&#3591;&#3588;&#3617;-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>29</span></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Unemployment</td>\n<td>1.2% <span style="font-size:85%;"> (2017)</span></td>\n<td style="text-align: right;"><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nesdb17_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-nesdb17-84">[84]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Total public debt</td>\n<td>&#3647;6.37 trillion <span style="font-size:85%;"> (<span class="nowrap">Dec.</span> 2017)</span></td>\n<td style="text-align: right;"><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Poverty</td>\n<td>8.61% <span style="font-size:85%;"> (2016)</span></td>\n<td style="text-align: right;"><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-&#3626;&#3633;&#3591;&#3588;&#3617;_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-&#3626;&#3633;&#3591;&#3588;&#3617;-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>36</span></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Net household worth</td>\n<td>&#3647;20.34 trillion <span style="font-size:85%;"> (2010)</span></td>\n<td style="text-align: right;"><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hhbot_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-hhbot-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>2</span></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Thailand is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emerging_markets" title="Emerging markets">emerging economy</a> and is considered a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newly_industrialised_country" title="Newly industrialised country">newly industrialised country</a>. Thailand had a 2017 GDP of US$1.236 trillion (on a <a href="/wiki/Purchasing_power_parity" title="Purchasing power parity">purchasing power parity</a> basis).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Thailand is the 2nd largest economy in Southeast Asia after Indonesia. Thailand ranks midway in the wealth spread in Southeast Asia as it is the 4th richest nation according to GDP per capita, after Singapore, Brunei, and Malaysia.\n</p><p>Thailand functions as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anchor_economy" title="Anchor economy">anchor economy</a> for the neighbouring developing economies of Laos, Myanmar, and Cambodia. In the third quarter of 2014, the unemployment rate in Thailand stood at 0.84% according to Thailand\'s National Economic and Social Development Board (NESDB).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_economic_history">Recent economic history</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MahaNakhon_by_kylehase.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5011" data-file-width="3623" decoding="async" height="304" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/MahaNakhon_by_kylehase.jpg/220px-MahaNakhon_by_kylehase.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/MahaNakhon_by_kylehase.jpg/330px-MahaNakhon_by_kylehase.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/MahaNakhon_by_kylehase.jpg/440px-MahaNakhon_by_kylehase.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MahaNakhon_by_kylehase.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MahaNakhon" title="MahaNakhon">MahaNakhon</a> skyscraper in Bangkok, once the tallest in Thailand</div></div></div>\n<p>Thailand experienced the world\'s highest economic growth rate from 1985 to 1996 &#8211; averaging 12.4% annually. In 1997 increased pressure on the <a href="/wiki/Thai_baht" title="Thai baht">baht</a>, a year in which the economy contracted by 1.9%, led to a crisis that uncovered financial sector weaknesses and forced the <a href="/wiki/Chavalit_Yongchaiyudh" title="Chavalit Yongchaiyudh">Chavalit Yongchaiyudh</a> administration to <a href="/wiki/Floating_exchange_rate" title="Floating exchange rate">float the currency</a>. Prime Minister Chavalit Yongchaiyudh was forced to resign after his cabinet came under fire for its slow response to the economic crisis. The baht was pegged at 25 to the US dollar from 1978 to 1997. The baht reached its lowest point of 56 to the US dollar in January 1998 and the economy contracted by 10.8% that year, triggering the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asian_financial_crisis" title="Asian financial crisis">Asian financial crisis</a>.\n</p><p>Thailand\'s economy started to recover in 1999, expanding 4.2&#8211;4.4% in 2000, thanks largely to strong exports. Growth (2.2%) was dampened by the softening of the global economy in 2001, but picked up in the subsequent years owing to strong growth in Asia, a relatively weak baht encouraging exports, and increased domestic spending as a result of several mega projects and incentives of Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Thaksin_Shinawatra" title="Thaksin Shinawatra">Thaksin Shinawatra</a>, known as <a href="/wiki/Thaksinomics" title="Thaksinomics">Thaksinomics</a>. Growth in 2002, 2003, and 2004 was 5&#8211;7% annually.\n</p><p>Growth in 2005, 2006, and 2007 hovered around 4&#8211;5%. Due both to the weakening of the US dollar and an increasingly strong Thai currency, by March 2008 the dollar was hovering around the 33 baht mark. While Thaksinomics has received criticism, official economic data reveals that between 2001 and 2011, Isan\'s GDP per capita more than doubled to US$1,475, while, over the same period, GDP in the Bangkok area increased from US$7,900 to nearly US$13,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>\n</p><p>With the instability surrounding major 2010 protests, the GDP growth of Thailand settled at around 4&#8211;5%, from highs of 5&#8211;7% under the previous civilian administration. Political uncertainty was identified as the primary cause of a decline in investor and consumer confidence. The IMF predicted that the Thai economy would rebound strongly from the low 0.1% GDP growth in 2011, to 5.5% in 2012 and then 7.5% in 2013, due to the monetary policy of the Bank of Thailand, as well as a package of fiscal stimulus measures introduced by the former <a href="/wiki/Yingluck_Shinawatra" title="Yingluck Shinawatra">Yingluck Shinawatra</a> government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Following the Thai military coup of 22 May 2014, the AFP global news agency published an article that claimed that the nation was on the verge of recession. The article focused on the departure of nearly 180,000 Cambodians from Thailand due to fears of an <a href="/wiki/Immigration" title="Immigration">immigration</a> clampdown, but concluded with information on the Thai economy\'s contraction of 2.1% quarter-on-quarter, from January to the end of March 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Income.2C_poverty_and_wealth"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Income,_poverty_and_wealth">Income, poverty and wealth</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iconsiam_at_Day_(III).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3551" data-file-width="5326" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Iconsiam_at_Day_%28III%29.jpg/220px-Iconsiam_at_Day_%28III%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Iconsiam_at_Day_%28III%29.jpg/330px-Iconsiam_at_Day_%28III%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Iconsiam_at_Day_%28III%29.jpg/440px-Iconsiam_at_Day_%28III%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iconsiam_at_Day_(III).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Inside of <a href="/wiki/Iconsiam" title="Iconsiam">Iconsiam</a>, one of the most luxurious shopping centre in <a href="/wiki/Bangkok" title="Bangkok">Bangkok</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Thais have median wealth per one adult person of $1,469 in 2016,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wealth16_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-wealth16-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>98</span></sup> increasing from $605 in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wealth16_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-wealth16-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>34</span></sup> In 2016, Thailand was <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_Human_Development_Index" title="List of countries by Human Development Index">ranked 87th in Human Development Index</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_inequality-adjusted_HDI" title="List of countries by inequality-adjusted HDI">70th in the inequality-adjusted HDI</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2017, Thailand\'s median household income was &#3647;26,946 per month.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nso60_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-nso60-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>1</span></sup> Top quintile households had a 45.0% share of all income, while bottom quintile households had 7.1%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nso60_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-nso60-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>4</span></sup> There were 26.9 million persons who had the bottom 40% of income earning less than &#3647;5,344 per person per month.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-poverty59_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-poverty59-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>5</span></sup> During <a href="/wiki/2013%E2%80%932014_Thai_political_crisis" title="2013&#8211;2014 Thai political crisis">2013&#8211;2014 Thai political crisis</a>, a survey found that anti-government <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Democratic_Reform_Committee" title="People\'s Democratic Reform Committee">PDRC</a> mostly (32%) had a monthly income of more than &#3647;50,000, while pro-government <a href="/wiki/United_Front_for_Democracy_Against_Dictatorship" title="United Front for Democracy Against Dictatorship">UDD</a> mostly (27%) had between &#3647;10,000 and &#3647;20,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>7</span></sup>\n</p><p>In 2014, <a href="/wiki/Credit_Suisse" title="Credit Suisse">Credit Suisse</a> reported that Thailand was the world\'s third most unequal country, behind Russia and India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbcworth17_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbcworth17-98">[98]</a></sup> Top 10% richest held 79% of the country\'s asset.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbcworth17_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-bbcworth17-98">[98]</a></sup> Top 1% richest held 58% worth of the economy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbcworth17_98-2"><a href="#cite_note-bbcworth17-98">[98]</a></sup> Thai 50 richest families had a total net worth accounting to 30% of GDP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbcworth17_98-3"><a href="#cite_note-bbcworth17-98">[98]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2016, 5.81 million people lived in poverty, or 11.6 million people (17.2% of population) if "near poor" is included.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-poverty59_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-poverty59-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>1</span></sup> Proportion of the poor relative to total population in each region was 12.96% in the Northeast, 12.35% in the South, and 9.83% in the North.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-poverty59_96-2"><a href="#cite_note-poverty59-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>2</span></sup> In 2017, there were 14 million people who applied for social welfare (yearly income of less than &#3647;100,000 was required).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbcworth17_98-4"><a href="#cite_note-bbcworth17-98">[98]</a></sup> At the end of 2017, Thailand\'s total household debt was &#3647;11.76 trillion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-&#3626;&#3633;&#3591;&#3588;&#3617;_85-2"><a href="#cite_note-&#3626;&#3633;&#3591;&#3588;&#3617;-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>5</span></sup> In 2010, 3% of all household were bankrupt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hhbot_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-hhbot-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>5</span></sup> In 2016, there were estimated 30,000 homeless persons in the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Exports_and_manufacturing">Exports and manufacturing</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thailand_Export_Treemap.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="751" data-file-width="962" decoding="async" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Thailand_Export_Treemap.png/250px-Thailand_Export_Treemap.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Thailand_Export_Treemap.png/375px-Thailand_Export_Treemap.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Thailand_Export_Treemap.png/500px-Thailand_Export_Treemap.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thailand_Export_Treemap.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A proportional representation of Thailand\'s exports</div></div></div>\n<p>The economy of Thailand is heavily export-dependent, with exports accounting for more than two-thirds of gross domestic product (GDP). Thailand exports over US$105 billion worth of goods and services annually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIA_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-1">[1]</a></sup> Major exports include cars, computers, electrical appliances, <a href="/wiki/Rice_production_in_Thailand" title="Rice production in Thailand">rice</a>, textiles and footwear, fishery products, rubber, and jewellery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIA_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-1">[1]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Substantial industries include electric appliances, components, computer components, and vehicles. Thailand\'s recovery from the 1997&#8211;1998 Asian financial crisis depended mainly on exports, among various other factors. As of  2012<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Thailand&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the <a href="/wiki/Automotive_industry_in_Thailand" title="Automotive industry in Thailand">Thai automotive industry</a> was the largest in <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_motor_vehicle_production" title="List of countries by motor vehicle production">9th largest in the world</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BkP_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-BkP-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OICA_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-OICA-102">[102]</a></sup> The Thailand industry has an annual output of near 1.5 million vehicles, mostly commercial vehicles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OICA_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-OICA-102">[102]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Most of the vehicles built in Thailand are developed and licensed by foreign producers, mainly <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japanese</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korean</a>. The Thai car industry takes advantage of the <a href="/wiki/ASEAN_Free_Trade_Area" title="ASEAN Free Trade Area">ASEAN Free Trade Area</a> (AFTA) to find a market for many of its products. Eight manufacturers, five Japanese, two US, and <a href="/wiki/Tata_Group" title="Tata Group">Tata</a> of India, produce pick-up trucks in Thailand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nikkei_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nikkei-103">[103]</a></sup> Thailand is the second largest consumer of pick-up trucks in the world, after the US.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In 2014, pick-ups accounted for 42% of all new vehicle sales in Thailand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nikkei_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nikkei-103">[103]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transportation">Transportation</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Transport_in_Thailand" title="Transport in Thailand">Transport in Thailand</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_airports_in_Thailand" title="List of airports in Thailand">List of airports in Thailand</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bangkok_Skytrain_2011.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2322" data-file-width="3497" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Bangkok_Skytrain_2011.jpg/220px-Bangkok_Skytrain_2011.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Bangkok_Skytrain_2011.jpg/330px-Bangkok_Skytrain_2011.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Bangkok_Skytrain_2011.jpg/440px-Bangkok_Skytrain_2011.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bangkok_Skytrain_2011.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/BTS_Skytrain" title="BTS Skytrain">BTS Skytrain</a> is an elevated rapid transit system in Bangkok</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Airbus_A380-841,_Thai_Airways_International_AN2253510.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="801" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Airbus_A380-841%2C_Thai_Airways_International_AN2253510.jpg/220px-Airbus_A380-841%2C_Thai_Airways_International_AN2253510.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Airbus_A380-841%2C_Thai_Airways_International_AN2253510.jpg/330px-Airbus_A380-841%2C_Thai_Airways_International_AN2253510.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Airbus_A380-841%2C_Thai_Airways_International_AN2253510.jpg/440px-Airbus_A380-841%2C_Thai_Airways_International_AN2253510.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Airbus_A380-841,_Thai_Airways_International_AN2253510.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A380" title="Airbus A380">Airbus A380</a> of the national carrier <a href="/wiki/Thai_Airways" title="Thai Airways">Thai Airways</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/State_Railway_of_Thailand" title="State Railway of Thailand">State Railway of Thailand</a> (SRT) operates all of Thailand\'s national rail lines. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bangkok_Railway_Station" title="Bangkok Railway Station">Bangkok Railway Station</a> (Hua Lamphong Station) is the main terminus of all routes. Phahonyothin and ICD <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lat_Krabang" title="Lat Krabang">Lat Krabang</a> are the main freight terminals.\n</p><p>As of  2017<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Thailand&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> SRT had 4,507&#160;km (2,801&#160;mi) of track, all of it <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meter_gauge" title="Meter gauge">meter gauge</a> except the Airport Link. Nearly all is single-track (4,097&#160;km), although some important sections around Bangkok are double (303&#160;km or 188&#160;mi) or triple-tracked (107&#160;km or 66&#160;mi) and there are plans to extend this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> By comparison, Thailand has 390,000&#160;km (242,335 miles) of highways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAR-20170123_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAR-20170123-105">[105]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Rail_transport_in_Bangkok" title="Rail transport in Bangkok">Rail transport in Bangkok</a> includes long-distance services, and some daily commuter trains running from and to the outskirts of the city during the rush hour, but passenger numbers have remained low. There are also three rapid transit rail systems in the capital.\n</p><p>As of 2012, Thailand had 103 airports with 63 paved runways, in addition to 6 heliports. The busiest airport in the county is Bangkok\'s <a href="/wiki/Suvarnabhumi_Airport" title="Suvarnabhumi Airport">Suvarnabhumi Airport</a>.\n</p><p>Thailand has 390,000&#160;km (242,335 miles) of highways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAR-20170123_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-NAR-20170123-105">[105]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a> Thailand has 462,133 roads and many multi-lane highways. As of  2017<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Thailand&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> Thailand has 37 million registered vehicles, 20 million of them motorbikes.\n</p><p>A number of undivided two-lane highways have been converted into divided four-lane highways. A Bangkok - <a href="/wiki/Chonburi_(city)" title="Chonburi (city)">Chon Buri</a> motorway (Route 7) now links to the new airport and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Seaboard_of_Thailand" title="Eastern Seaboard of Thailand">Eastern Seaboard</a>.\n</p><p>Other forms of road transport includes <a href="/wiki/Auto_rickshaw" title="Auto rickshaw">tuk-tuks</a>, taxis&#8212;as of November 2018, Thailand has 80,647 registered taxis nationwide<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>&#8212;vans (<a href="/wiki/Minibus" title="Minibus">minibus</a>), motorbike taxis, and <a href="/wiki/Songthaew" title="Songthaew">songthaews</a>.\n</p><p>There are 4,125 public vans operating on 114 routes from Bangkok to the provinces alone. They are classed as Category 2 public transport vehicles (routes within 300 kilometres). Until 2016, most operated from a Bangkok terminus at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Victory_Monument_(Thailand)" title="Victory Monument (Thailand)">Victory Monument</a>. They are being moved from there to the Department of Land Transport\'s three Bangkok bus terminals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tourism">Tourism</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Tourism_in_Thailand" title="Tourism in Thailand">Tourism in Thailand</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Three_Chedis,_Wat_Phra_Kaew.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5435" data-file-width="3623" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/The_Three_Chedis%2C_Wat_Phra_Kaew.jpg/220px-The_Three_Chedis%2C_Wat_Phra_Kaew.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/The_Three_Chedis%2C_Wat_Phra_Kaew.jpg/330px-The_Three_Chedis%2C_Wat_Phra_Kaew.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/The_Three_Chedis%2C_Wat_Phra_Kaew.jpg/440px-The_Three_Chedis%2C_Wat_Phra_Kaew.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Three_Chedis,_Wat_Phra_Kaew.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Wat_Phra_Kaew" title="Wat Phra Kaew">Wat Phra Kaew</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bangkok" title="Bangkok">Bangkok</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Tourism makes up about 6% of the country\'s economy. Thailand was the most visited country in Southeast Asia in 2013, according to the World Tourism Organisation.\nEstimates of tourism receipts directly contributing to the Thai GDP of 12 trillion baht range from 9 percent (1 trillion baht) (2013) to 16 percent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> When including the indirect effects of tourism, it is said to account for 20.2 percent (2.4 trillion baht) of Thailand\'s GDP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WTTC-2014_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-WTTC-2014-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>1</span></sup>\n</p><p>The Tourism Authority of Thailand (TAT) uses the slogan "Amazing Thailand" to promote Thailand internationally. In 2015, this was supplemented by a "Discover Thainess" campaign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TAT-History_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-TAT-History-110">[110]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Asian tourists primarily visit Thailand for Bangkok and the historical, natural, and cultural sights in its vicinity. Western tourists not only visit Bangkok and surroundings, but in addition many travel to the southern beaches and islands. The north is the chief destination for trekking and <a href="/wiki/Adventure_travel" title="Adventure travel">adventure travel</a> with its diverse ethnic minority groups and forested mountains. The region hosting the fewest tourists is <a href="/wiki/Isan" title="Isan">Isan</a> in the northeast. To accommodate foreign visitors, the Thai government established a separate tourism police with offices in the major tourist areas and its own central emergency telephone number.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maya_Bay,_Thailand_by_Mike_Clegg_Photography.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2828" data-file-width="4242" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Maya_Bay%2C_Thailand_by_Mike_Clegg_Photography.jpg/220px-Maya_Bay%2C_Thailand_by_Mike_Clegg_Photography.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Maya_Bay%2C_Thailand_by_Mike_Clegg_Photography.jpg/330px-Maya_Bay%2C_Thailand_by_Mike_Clegg_Photography.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Maya_Bay%2C_Thailand_by_Mike_Clegg_Photography.jpg/440px-Maya_Bay%2C_Thailand_by_Mike_Clegg_Photography.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maya_Bay,_Thailand_by_Mike_Clegg_Photography.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Maya Bay Beach, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phuket" title="Phuket">Phuket</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Thailand\'s attractions include <a href="/wiki/Underwater_diving" title="Underwater diving">diving</a>, sandy beaches, <a href="/wiki/List_of_islands_of_Thailand" title="List of islands of Thailand">hundreds of tropical islands</a>, nightlife, archaeological sites, museums, <a href="/wiki/Hill_tribe_(Thailand)" title="Hill tribe (Thailand)">hill tribes</a>, flora and bird life, palaces, <a href="/wiki/List_of_Buddhist_temples_in_Thailand" title="List of Buddhist temples in Thailand">Buddhist temples</a> and several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_World_Heritage_Sites_in_Asia_and_Australasia#Thailand" title="List of World Heritage Sites in Asia and Australasia">World Heritage</a> sites. Many tourists follow courses during their stay in Thailand. Popular are classes in Thai cooking, <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> and traditional <a href="/wiki/Thai_massage" title="Thai massage">Thai massage</a>. Thai national festivals range from Thai New Year <a href="/wiki/Songkran_(Thailand)" title="Songkran (Thailand)">Songkran</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Loy_Krathong" title="Loy Krathong">Loy Krathong</a>. Many localities in Thailand also have their own festivals. Among the best-known are the "<a href="/wiki/Surin_Elephant_Round-up" title="Surin Elephant Round-up">Elephant Round-up</a>" in <a href="/wiki/Surin,_Thailand" title="Surin, Thailand">Surin</a>, the "<a href="/wiki/Rocket_Festival" title="Rocket Festival">Rocket Festival</a>" in <a href="/wiki/Yasothon" title="Yasothon">Yasothon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Suwannaphum_District" title="Suwannaphum District">Suwannaphum District</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phanom_Phrai_District" title="Phanom Phrai District">Phanom Phrai District</a> both district are located in <a href="/wiki/Roi_Et_Province" title="Roi Et Province">Roi Et Province</a> and the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pee_Ta_Khon" title="Pee Ta Khon">Phi Ta Khon</a>" festival in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amphoe_Dan_Sai" title="Amphoe Dan Sai">Dan Sai</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cuisine_of_Thailand" title="Cuisine of Thailand">Thai cuisine</a> has become famous worldwide with its enthusiastic use of fresh herbs and spices.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iconsiam_shopping_mall.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1152" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Iconsiam_shopping_mall.jpg/220px-Iconsiam_shopping_mall.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Iconsiam_shopping_mall.jpg/330px-Iconsiam_shopping_mall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Iconsiam_shopping_mall.jpg/440px-Iconsiam_shopping_mall.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iconsiam_shopping_mall.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Iconsiam" title="Iconsiam">Iconsiam</a> shopping complex, as seen from across the <a href="/wiki/Chao_Phraya_River" title="Chao Phraya River">Chao Phraya River</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Bangkok <a href="/wiki/List_of_shopping_malls_in_Thailand" title="List of shopping malls in Thailand">shopping malls</a> offer a variety of international and local brands. Towards the north of the city, and easily reached by <a href="/wiki/BTS_Skytrain" title="BTS Skytrain">skytrain</a> or <a href="/wiki/MRT_(Bangkok)" title="MRT (Bangkok)">underground</a>, is the <a href="/wiki/Chatuchak_Weekend_Market" title="Chatuchak Weekend Market">Chatuchak Weekend Market</a>. It is possibly the largest market in the world, selling everything from household items to live, and sometimes endangered, animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> The "<a href="/wiki/Pratunam_Market" title="Pratunam Market">Pratunam Market</a>" specialises in fabrics and clothing. The night markets in the <a href="/wiki/Si_Lom" title="Si Lom">Silom</a> area and on <a href="/wiki/Khaosan_Road" title="Khaosan Road">Khaosan Road</a> are mainly tourist-oriented, selling items such as T-shirts, handicrafts, counterfeit watches and sunglasses. In the vicinity of Bangkok one can find several <a href="/wiki/Floating_market" title="Floating market">floating markets</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Damnoen_Saduak_Floating_Market" title="Damnoen Saduak Floating Market">the one in Damnoen Saduak</a>. The "Sunday Evening Walking Street Market", held on Rachadamnoen Road inside the old city, is a shopping highlight of a visit to <a href="/wiki/Chiang_Mai" title="Chiang Mai">Chiang Mai</a> up in northern Thailand. It attracts many locals as well as foreigners. The "Night Bazaar" is Chiang Mai\'s more tourist-oriented market, sprawling over several city blocks just east of the old city walls towards the river.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Prostitution_in_Thailand" title="Prostitution in Thailand">Prostitution in Thailand</a> and sex tourism also form a <i>de facto</i> part of the economy. Campaigns promote Thailand as exotic to attract tourists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> Cultural milieu combined with poverty and the lure of money have caused prostitution and sex tourism in particular to flourish in Thailand. One estimate published in 2003 placed the trade at US$4.3 billion per year or about 3% of the Thai economy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-legal_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-legal-114">[114]</a></sup> According to research by <a href="/wiki/Chulalongkorn_University" title="Chulalongkorn University">Chulalongkorn University</a> on the Thai illegal economy, prostitution in Thailand in the period between 1993 and 1995, made up around 2.7% of the GDP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> It is believed that at least 10% of tourist dollars are spent on the sex trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Thailand is at the forefront of the growing practice of sex-reassignment surgery (SRS). Statistic taken from 2014, illustrated the country\'s medical tourism industry attracting over 2.5 million visitors per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> In 2017 and 2018 Thailand saw 2.4 million and 2.5 million medical tourists, respectively, with data showing more modern forms of cosmetic surgery growing in popularity<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>. In 1985&#8211;1990, only 5% of foreign transsexual patients visited Thailand for sex-reassignment surgery. In more recent years, 2010&#8211;2012, more than 90% of the visitors traveled to Thailand for SRS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agriculture">Agriculture</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_Thailand" title="Agriculture in Thailand">Agriculture in Thailand</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rice_farmers_Mae_Wang_Chiang_Mai_Province.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1316" data-file-width="2560" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Rice_farmers_Mae_Wang_Chiang_Mai_Province.jpg/300px-Rice_farmers_Mae_Wang_Chiang_Mai_Province.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Rice_farmers_Mae_Wang_Chiang_Mai_Province.jpg/450px-Rice_farmers_Mae_Wang_Chiang_Mai_Province.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Rice_farmers_Mae_Wang_Chiang_Mai_Province.jpg/600px-Rice_farmers_Mae_Wang_Chiang_Mai_Province.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rice_farmers_Mae_Wang_Chiang_Mai_Province.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Thailand has long been <a href="/wiki/Rice_production_in_Thailand" title="Rice production in Thailand">one of the largest rice exporters in the world</a>. Forty-nine percent of Thailand\'s labour force is employed in agriculture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODI1_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-ODI1-120">[120]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Forty-nine per cent of Thailand\'s labour force is employed in <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_Thailand" title="Agriculture in Thailand">agriculture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODI1_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-ODI1-120">[120]</a></sup> This is down from 70% in 1980.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODI1_120-2"><a href="#cite_note-ODI1-120">[120]</a></sup> Rice is the most important crop in the country and Thailand had long been the world\'s leading exporter of rice, until recently falling behind both India and Vietnam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> Thailand has the highest percentage of arable land, 27.25%, of any nation in the <a href="/wiki/Greater_Mekong_Subregion" title="Greater Mekong Subregion">Greater Mekong Subregion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> About 55% of the arable land area is used for rice production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IRRI_Thailand_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-IRRI_Thailand-123">[123]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Agriculture has been experiencing a transition from labour-intensive and transitional methods to a more industrialised and competitive sector.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODI1_120-3"><a href="#cite_note-ODI1-120">[120]</a></sup> Between 1962 and 1983, the agricultural sector grew by 4.1% per year on average and continued to grow at 2.2% between 1983 and 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODI1_120-4"><a href="#cite_note-ODI1-120">[120]</a></sup> The relative contribution of agriculture to GDP has declined while exports of goods and services have increased.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy">Energy</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Energy_in_Thailand" title="Energy in Thailand">Energy in Thailand</a></div>\n<p>75% of Thailand\'s electrical generation is powered by <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> in 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Energy_Security_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Energy_Security-124">[124]</a></sup> Coal-fired power plants produce an additional 20% of electricity, with the remainder coming from biomass, hydro, and biogas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Energy_Security_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-Energy_Security-124">[124]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Thailand produces roughly one-third of the oil it consumes. It is the second largest importer of oil in SE Asia. Thailand is a large producer of natural gas, with reserves of at least 10 trillion cubic feet. After Indonesia, it is the largest coal producer in SE Asia, but must import additional coal to meet domestic demand.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Informal_economy">Informal economy</span></h3>\n<p>Thailand has an diverse and robust informal labor sector&#8212;in 2012, it was estimated that informal workers comprised 62.6% of the Thai workforce. The Ministry of Labor defines informal workers to be individuals who work in informal economies and do not have employee status under a given country\'s Labor Protection Act (LPA). The informal sector in Thailand has grown significantly over the past 60 years over the course of Thailand\'s gradual transition from an agriculture-based economy to becoming more industrialized and service-oriented.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-125">[125]</a></sup> Between 1993&#8211;1995, ten percent of the Thai labor force moved from the agricultural sector to urban and industrial jobs, especially in the manufacturing sector. It is estimated that between 1988&#8211;1995, the number of factory workers in the country doubled from two to four million, as Thailand\'s GDP tripled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-:6-126">[126]</a></sup> While the Asian Financial Crisis that followed in 1997 hit the Thai economy hard, the industrial sector continued to expand under widespread deregulation, as Thailand was mandated to adopt a range of structural adjustment reforms upon receiving funding from the IMF and World Bank. These reforms implemented an agenda of increased privatization and trade liberalization in the country, and decreased federal subsidization of public goods and utilities, agricultural price supports, and regulations on fair wages and labor conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-127">[127]</a></sup> These changes put further pressure on the agricultural sector, and prompted continued migration from the rural countryside to the growing cities. Many migrant farmers found work in Thailand\'s growing manufacturing industry, and took jobs in sweatshops and factories with few labor regulations and often exploitative conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Those that could not find formal factory work, including illegal migrants and the families of rural Thai migrants that followed their relatives to the urban centers, turned to the informal sector to provide the extra support needed for survival&#8212;under the widespread regulation imposed by the structural adjustment programs, one family member working in a factory or sweatshop made very little. Scholars argue that the economic consequences and social costs of Thailand\'s labor reforms in the wake of the 1997 Asian Financial Crisis fell on individuals and families rather than the state. This can be described as the "externalization of market risk", meaning that as the country\'s labor market became increasingly deregulated, the burden and responsibility of providing an adequate livelihood shifted from employers and the state to the workers themselves, whose families had to find jobs in the informal sector to make up for the losses and subsidize the wages being made by their relatives in the formal sector. The weight of these economic changes hit migrants and the urban poor especially hard, and the informal sector expanded rapidly as a result.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-127">[127]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Today, informal labor in Thailand is typically broken down into three main groups: subcontracted/self employed/home-based workers, service workers (including those that are employed in restaurants, as street vendors, masseuses, taxi drivers, and as domestic workers), and agricultural workers. Not included in these categories are those that work in entertainment, nightlife, and the sex industry. Individuals employed in these facets of the informal labor sector face additional vulnerabilities, including recruitment into circles of sexual exploitation and human trafficking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-125">[125]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In general, education levels are low in the informal sector. A 2012 study found that 64% of informal workers had not completed education beyond primary school. Many informal workers are also migrants, only some of which have legal status in the country. Education and citizenship are two main barriers to entry for those looking to work in formal industries, and enjoy the labor protections and social security benefits that come along with formal employment. Because the informal labor sector is not recognized under the Labor Protection Act (LPA), informal workers are much more vulnerable labor to exploitation and unsafe working conditions than those employed in more formal and federally recognized industries. While some Thai labor laws provide minimal protections to domestic and agricultural workers, they are often weak and difficult to enforce. Furthermore, Thai social security policies fail to protect against the risks many informal workers face, including workplace accidents and compensation as well as unemployment and retirement insurance. Many informal workers are not legally contracted for their employment, and many do not make a living wage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_125-2"><a href="#cite_note-:2-125">[125]</a></sup> As a result, labor trafficking is common in the region, affecting children and adults, men and women, and migrants and Thai citizens alike.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Thailand" title="Demographics of Thailand">Demographics of Thailand</a></div>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bev%C3%B6lkerungspyramide_Thailand_2016.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="425" data-file-width="610" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Bev%C3%B6lkerungspyramide_Thailand_2016.png/220px-Bev%C3%B6lkerungspyramide_Thailand_2016.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Bev%C3%B6lkerungspyramide_Thailand_2016.png/330px-Bev%C3%B6lkerungspyramide_Thailand_2016.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Bev%C3%B6lkerungspyramide_Thailand_2016.png/440px-Bev%C3%B6lkerungspyramide_Thailand_2016.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bev%C3%B6lkerungspyramide_Thailand_2016.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Population pyramid 2016</div></div></div>\n<table class="wikitable" style="float: right; margin-left: 10px">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th colspan="4" style="text-align:center; background:#cfb;">Population in Thailand<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP-8">[8]</a></sup>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<th style="background:#cfb;">Year\n</th>\n<th style="background:#cfb;">Million\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="text-align:left;">1950</td>\n<td style="text-align:right;">20.7\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="text-align:left;">2000</td>\n<td style="text-align:right;">62.9\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="text-align:left;">2016</td>\n<td style="text-align:right;">68.9\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Thailand had a population of 68,863,514<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP-8">[8]</a></sup> as of  2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Thailand&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>. Thailand\'s population is largely rural, concentrated in the rice-growing areas of the central, northeastern, and northern regions. About 45.7% of Thailand\'s population lived in urban areas as of  2010<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Thailand&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, concentrated mostly in and around the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bangkok_Metropolitan_Area" title="Bangkok Metropolitan Area">Bangkok Metropolitan Area</a>.\n</p><p>Thailand\'s government-sponsored family planning program resulted in a dramatic decline in population growth from 3.1% in 1960 to around 0.4% today. In 1970, an average of 5.7 people lived in a Thai household. At the time of the 2010 census, the average Thai household size was 3.2 people.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethnic_groups">Ethnic groups</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups_in_Thailand" title="Ethnic groups in Thailand">Ethnic groups in Thailand</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wat_phra_mahathat_woramahawihan_nakhon_si_thammarat.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3456" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Wat_phra_mahathat_woramahawihan_nakhon_si_thammarat.jpg/220px-Wat_phra_mahathat_woramahawihan_nakhon_si_thammarat.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Wat_phra_mahathat_woramahawihan_nakhon_si_thammarat.jpg/330px-Wat_phra_mahathat_woramahawihan_nakhon_si_thammarat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Wat_phra_mahathat_woramahawihan_nakhon_si_thammarat.jpg/440px-Wat_phra_mahathat_woramahawihan_nakhon_si_thammarat.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wat_phra_mahathat_woramahawihan_nakhon_si_thammarat.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A procession during the <i>Hae Pha Khuen That</i> festival of <a href="/wiki/Wat_Phra_Mahathat" title="Wat Phra Mahathat">Wat Phra Mahathat</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Thai nationals make up the majority of Thailand\'s population, 95.9% in 2010. The remaining 4.1% of the population are Burmese (2.0%), others 1.3%, and unspecified 0.9%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIA_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-1">[1]</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to the Royal Thai Government\'s 2011 Country Report to the UN Committee responsible for the <a href="/wiki/International_Convention_on_the_Elimination_of_All_Forms_of_Racial_Discrimination" title="International Convention on the Elimination of All Forms of Racial Discrimination">International Convention for the Elimination of All Forms of Racial Discrimination</a>, available from the Department of Rights and Liberties Promotion of the Thai Ministry of Justice,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CERD/C/THA/1-3_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-CERD/C/THA/1-3-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>3</span></sup> 62 ethnic communities are officially recognised in Thailand. Twenty million Central Thai (together with approximately 650,000 <a href="/wiki/Khorat_Thai" title="Khorat Thai">Khorat Thai</a>) make up approximately 20,650,000 (34.1 percent) of the nation\'s population of 60,544,937<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> at the time of completion of the Mahidol University <i>Ethnolinguistic Maps of Thailand</i> data (1997).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EMT_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-EMT-130">[130]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E0%B8%99%E0%B8%B2%E0%B8%A2%E0%B8%81%E0%B8%A3%E0%B8%B1%E0%B8%90%E0%B8%A1%E0%B8%99%E0%B8%95%E0%B8%A3%E0%B8%B5_%E0%B8%A1%E0%B8%AD%E0%B8%9A%E0%B8%9A%E0%B9%89%E0%B8%B2%E0%B8%99%E0%B8%95%E0%B8%B2%E0%B8%A1%E0%B9%82%E0%B8%84%E0%B8%A3%E0%B8%87%E0%B8%81%E0%B8%B2%E0%B8%A3%E0%B9%81%E0%B8%81%E0%B9%89%E0%B9%84%E0%B8%82%E0%B8%9B%E0%B8%B1%E0%B8%8D%E0%B8%AB%E0%B8%B2%E0%B8%84%E0%B8%A7%E0%B8%B2%E0%B8%A1%E0%B9%80%E0%B8%94%E0%B8%B7%E0%B8%AD%E0%B8%94%E0%B8%A3%E0%B9%89%E0%B8%AD%E0%B8%99%E0%B8%97_-_Flickr_-_Abhisit_Vejjajiva_(10).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3413" data-file-width="5120" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/%E0%B8%99%E0%B8%B2%E0%B8%A2%E0%B8%81%E0%B8%A3%E0%B8%B1%E0%B8%90%E0%B8%A1%E0%B8%99%E0%B8%95%E0%B8%A3%E0%B8%B5_%E0%B8%A1%E0%B8%AD%E0%B8%9A%E0%B8%9A%E0%B9%89%E0%B8%B2%E0%B8%99%E0%B8%95%E0%B8%B2%E0%B8%A1%E0%B9%82%E0%B8%84%E0%B8%A3%E0%B8%87%E0%B8%81%E0%B8%B2%E0%B8%A3%E0%B9%81%E0%B8%81%E0%B9%89%E0%B9%84%E0%B8%82%E0%B8%9B%E0%B8%B1%E0%B8%8D%E0%B8%AB%E0%B8%B2%E0%B8%84%E0%B8%A7%E0%B8%B2%E0%B8%A1%E0%B9%80%E0%B8%94%E0%B8%B7%E0%B8%AD%E0%B8%94%E0%B8%A3%E0%B9%89%E0%B8%AD%E0%B8%99%E0%B8%97_-_Flickr_-_Abhisit_Vejjajiva_%2810%29.jpg/220px-thumbnail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/%E0%B8%99%E0%B8%B2%E0%B8%A2%E0%B8%81%E0%B8%A3%E0%B8%B1%E0%B8%90%E0%B8%A1%E0%B8%99%E0%B8%95%E0%B8%A3%E0%B8%B5_%E0%B8%A1%E0%B8%AD%E0%B8%9A%E0%B8%9A%E0%B9%89%E0%B8%B2%E0%B8%99%E0%B8%95%E0%B8%B2%E0%B8%A1%E0%B9%82%E0%B8%84%E0%B8%A3%E0%B8%87%E0%B8%81%E0%B8%B2%E0%B8%A3%E0%B9%81%E0%B8%81%E0%B9%89%E0%B9%84%E0%B8%82%E0%B8%9B%E0%B8%B1%E0%B8%8D%E0%B8%AB%E0%B8%B2%E0%B8%84%E0%B8%A7%E0%B8%B2%E0%B8%A1%E0%B9%80%E0%B8%94%E0%B8%B7%E0%B8%AD%E0%B8%94%E0%B8%A3%E0%B9%89%E0%B8%AD%E0%B8%99%E0%B8%97_-_Flickr_-_Abhisit_Vejjajiva_%2810%29.jpg/330px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/%E0%B8%99%E0%B8%B2%E0%B8%A2%E0%B8%81%E0%B8%A3%E0%B8%B1%E0%B8%90%E0%B8%A1%E0%B8%99%E0%B8%95%E0%B8%A3%E0%B8%B5_%E0%B8%A1%E0%B8%AD%E0%B8%9A%E0%B8%9A%E0%B9%89%E0%B8%B2%E0%B8%99%E0%B8%95%E0%B8%B2%E0%B8%A1%E0%B9%82%E0%B8%84%E0%B8%A3%E0%B8%87%E0%B8%81%E0%B8%B2%E0%B8%A3%E0%B9%81%E0%B8%81%E0%B9%89%E0%B9%84%E0%B8%82%E0%B8%9B%E0%B8%B1%E0%B8%8D%E0%B8%AB%E0%B8%B2%E0%B8%84%E0%B8%A7%E0%B8%B2%E0%B8%A1%E0%B9%80%E0%B8%94%E0%B8%B7%E0%B8%AD%E0%B8%94%E0%B8%A3%E0%B9%89%E0%B8%AD%E0%B8%99%E0%B8%97_-_Flickr_-_Abhisit_Vejjajiva_%2810%29.jpg/440px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E0%B8%99%E0%B8%B2%E0%B8%A2%E0%B8%81%E0%B8%A3%E0%B8%B1%E0%B8%90%E0%B8%A1%E0%B8%99%E0%B8%95%E0%B8%A3%E0%B8%B5_%E0%B8%A1%E0%B8%AD%E0%B8%9A%E0%B8%9A%E0%B9%89%E0%B8%B2%E0%B8%99%E0%B8%95%E0%B8%B2%E0%B8%A1%E0%B9%82%E0%B8%84%E0%B8%A3%E0%B8%87%E0%B8%81%E0%B8%B2%E0%B8%A3%E0%B9%81%E0%B8%81%E0%B9%89%E0%B9%84%E0%B8%82%E0%B8%9B%E0%B8%B1%E0%B8%8D%E0%B8%AB%E0%B8%B2%E0%B8%84%E0%B8%A7%E0%B8%B2%E0%B8%A1%E0%B9%80%E0%B8%94%E0%B8%B7%E0%B8%AD%E0%B8%94%E0%B8%A3%E0%B9%89%E0%B8%AD%E0%B8%99%E0%B8%97_-_Flickr_-_Abhisit_Vejjajiva_(10).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Thai_Malays" title="Thai Malays">Thai Malays</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The 2011 Thailand Country Report provides population numbers for mountain peoples (\'hill tribes\') and ethnic communities in the Northeast and is explicit about its main reliance on the Mahidol University Ethnolinguistic Maps of Thailand data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EMT_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-EMT-130">[130]</a></sup> Thus, though over 3.288 million people in the Northeast alone could not be categorised, the population and percentages of other ethnic communities circa 1997 are known for all of Thailand and constitute minimum populations. In descending order, the largest (equal to or greater than 400,000) are a) 15,080,000 Lao (24.9 percent) consisting of the Thai Lao<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DraperKamnuansilpa_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-DraperKamnuansilpa-2">[2]</a></sup> (14 million) and other smaller Lao groups, namely the Thai Loei (400&#8211;500,000), Lao Lom (350,000), Lao Wiang/Klang (200,000), Lao Khrang (90,000), Lao Ngaew (30,000), and Lao Ti (10,000; b) six million Khon Muang (9.9 percent, also called Northern Thais); c) 4.5 million Pak Tai (7.5 percent, also called Southern Thais); d) 1.4 million Khmer Leu (2.3 percent, also called Northern Khmer); e) 900,000 Malay (1.5%); f) 500,000 Ngaw (0.8 percent); g) 470,000 Phu Thai (0.8 percent); h) 400,000 Kuy/Kuay (also known as Suay) (0.7 percent), and i) 350,000 Karen (0.6 percent).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CERD/C/THA/1-3_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-CERD/C/THA/1-3-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>7&#8211;13</span></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thai_Chinese" title="Thai Chinese">Thai Chinese</a>, those of significant Chinese heritage, are 14% of the population,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-West794_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-West794-6">[6]</a></sup> while Thais with partial Chinese ancestry comprise up to 40% of the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Theraphan_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-Theraphan-131">[131]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thai_Malays" title="Thai Malays">Thai Malays</a> represent 3% of the population, with the remainder consisting of <a href="/wiki/Mon_people" title="Mon people">Mons</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khmers" title="Khmers">Khmers</a> and various "<a href="/wiki/Hill_tribe_(Thailand)" title="Hill tribe (Thailand)">hill tribes</a>". The country\'s official language is <a href="/wiki/Thai_language" title="Thai language">Thai</a> and the primary religion is <a href="/wiki/Theravada" title="Theravada">Theravada</a> Buddhism, which is practised by around 95% of the population.\n</p><p>Increasing numbers of migrants from neighbouring Myanmar, Laos, and Cambodia, as well as from Nepal and India, have pushed the total number of non-national residents to around 3.5 million as of  2009<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Thailand&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, up from an estimated 2 million in 2008, and about 1.3 million in 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> Some 41,000 Britons live in Thailand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Population_centres">Population centres</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_cities_in_Thailand" title="List of cities in Thailand">List of cities in Thailand</a></div>\n<p><br/>\n</p>\n<table class="navbox">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th class="navbox-title" id="Language">Language</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Thailand" title="Languages of Thailand">Languages of Thailand</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ethnolinguistic_groups_of_Thailand_1974.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="932" data-file-width="601" decoding="async" height="264" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Ethnolinguistic_groups_of_Thailand_1974.png/170px-Ethnolinguistic_groups_of_Thailand_1974.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Ethnolinguistic_groups_of_Thailand_1974.png/255px-Ethnolinguistic_groups_of_Thailand_1974.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Ethnolinguistic_groups_of_Thailand_1974.png/340px-Ethnolinguistic_groups_of_Thailand_1974.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ethnolinguistic_groups_of_Thailand_1974.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An ethnolinguistic map of Thailand.</div></div></div>\n<p>The official language of Thailand is <a href="/wiki/Thai_language" title="Thai language">Thai</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tai%E2%80%93Kadai_languages" title="Tai&#8211;Kadai languages">Tai&#8211;Kadai language</a> closely related to <a href="/wiki/Lao_language" title="Lao language">Lao</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shan_language" title="Shan language">Shan</a> in Myanmar, and numerous smaller languages spoken in an arc from <a href="/wiki/Hainan" title="Hainan">Hainan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yunnan" title="Yunnan">Yunnan</a> south to the Chinese border. It is the principal language of education and government and spoken throughout the country. The standard is based on the dialect of the central Thai people, and it is written in the <a href="/wiki/Thai_alphabet" title="Thai alphabet">Thai alphabet</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Abugida" title="Abugida">abugida</a> script that evolved from the <a href="/wiki/Khmer_alphabet" title="Khmer alphabet">Khmer alphabet</a>.\n</p><p>Sixty-two languages were recognised by the Royal Thai Government in the 2011 Country Report to the UN Committee responsible for the <a href="/wiki/International_Convention_on_the_Elimination_of_All_Forms_of_Racial_Discrimination" title="International Convention on the Elimination of All Forms of Racial Discrimination"><i>International Convention for the Elimination of All Forms of Racial Discrimination</i></a>, which employed an ethnolinguistic approach and is available from the Department of Rights and Liberties Promotion of the Thai Ministry of Justice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CERD/C/THA/1-3_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-CERD/C/THA/1-3-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>3</span></sup> <a href="/wiki/Southern_Thai_language" title="Southern Thai language">Southern Thai</a> is spoken in the southern provinces, and <a href="/wiki/Northern_Thai_language" title="Northern Thai language">Northern Thai</a> is spoken in the provinces that were formerly part of the independent kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Lan_Na" title="Lan Na">Lan Na</a>. For the purposes of the national census, which does not recognise all 62 languages recognised by the Royal Thai Government in the 2011 Country Report, four dialects of Thai exist; these partly coincide with regional designations.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bangkok_National_Museum_-_2017-04-22_(008).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4032" data-file-width="3024" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Bangkok_National_Museum_-_2017-04-22_%28008%29.jpg/220px-Bangkok_National_Museum_-_2017-04-22_%28008%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Bangkok_National_Museum_-_2017-04-22_%28008%29.jpg/330px-Bangkok_National_Museum_-_2017-04-22_%28008%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Bangkok_National_Museum_-_2017-04-22_%28008%29.jpg/440px-Bangkok_National_Museum_-_2017-04-22_%28008%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bangkok_National_Museum_-_2017-04-22_(008).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Silajaruek of <a href="/wiki/Sukhothai_Kingdom" title="Sukhothai Kingdom">Sukhothai Kingdom</a> are hundreds of stone inscriptions that form a historical record of the period.</div></div></div>\n<p>The largest of Thailand\'s minority languages is the <a href="/wiki/Lao_language" title="Lao language">Lao</a> dialect of <a href="/wiki/Isan_language" title="Isan language">Isan</a> spoken in the northeastern provinces. Although sometimes considered a Thai dialect, it is a Lao dialect, and the region where it is traditionally spoken was historically part of the Lao kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Lan_Xang" title="Lan Xang">Lan Xang</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In the far south, <a href="/wiki/Kelantan-Pattani_Malay" title="Kelantan-Pattani Malay">Kelantan-Pattani Malay</a> is the primary language of Malay Muslims. Varieties of Chinese are also spoken by the large <a href="/wiki/Thai_Chinese" title="Thai Chinese">Thai Chinese</a> population, with the <a href="/wiki/Teochew_dialect" title="Teochew dialect">Teochew dialect</a> best-represented.\n</p><p>Numerous tribal languages are also spoken, including many <a href="/wiki/Austroasiatic_languages" title="Austroasiatic languages">Austroasiatic languages</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Mon_language" title="Mon language">Mon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Khmer_language" title="Khmer language">Khmer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_language" title="Vietnamese language">Viet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mlabri_language" title="Mlabri language">Mlabri</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aslian_languages" title="Aslian languages">Orang Asli</a>; <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_languages" title="Austronesian languages">Austronesian languages</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Cham_language" title="Cham language">Cham</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moken_language" title="Moken language">Moken</a>; <a href="/wiki/Sino-Tibetan_languages" title="Sino-Tibetan languages">Sino-Tibetan languages</a> like <a href="/wiki/Lawa_language" title="Lawa language">Lawa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Akha_language" title="Akha language">Akha</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Karen_languages" title="Karen languages">Karen</a>; and other <a href="/wiki/Tai_languages" title="Tai languages">Tai languages</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Tai_Yo_language" title="Tai Yo language">Tai Yo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phu_Thai_language" title="Phu Thai language">Phu Thai</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Saek_language" title="Saek language">Saek</a>. <a href="/wiki/Hmong_language" title="Hmong language">Hmong</a> is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Hmong%E2%80%93Mien_languages" title="Hmong&#8211;Mien languages">Hmong&#8211;Mien languages</a>, which is now regarded as a language family of its own.\n</p><p>English is a mandatory school subject, but the number of fluent speakers remains low, especially outside cities.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Thailand" title="Religion in Thailand">Religion in Thailand</a></div>\n<div class="barbox tright" style="overflow-x: auto;">\n<div style="border:1px solid silver; font-size:88%; padding:0.4em; width:auto; background: white;">\n<table style="text-align:left; border-collapse:collapse; width:100%;">\n<tbody><tr style="background:#ddd"><th colspan="5" style="text-align:center;">Religion in Thailand (2015)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto-7">[7]</a></sup></th></tr>\n<tr style="font-size:88%; height:4px;">\n<td style="width: 100px; border-left: solid 1px silver; border-right: solid 1px silver;"><div style="background:grey; width:0.01%; overflow: hidden;">&#8201;</div></td>\n<td colspan="2" style="padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 0.4em; text-align: right;">0.01%</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody></table>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Phutthamonthon_Buddha.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Phutthamonthon_Buddha.JPG/220px-Phutthamonthon_Buddha.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Phutthamonthon_Buddha.JPG/330px-Phutthamonthon_Buddha.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Phutthamonthon_Buddha.JPG/440px-Phutthamonthon_Buddha.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Phutthamonthon_Buddha.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theravada_Buddhism" title="Theravada Buddhism">Theravada Buddhism</a>, highly practised in Thailand</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Buddhist_offerings.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3692" data-file-width="2838" decoding="async" height="260" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Buddhist_offerings.jpg/200px-Buddhist_offerings.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Buddhist_offerings.jpg/300px-Buddhist_offerings.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Buddhist_offerings.jpg/400px-Buddhist_offerings.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Buddhist_offerings.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Worshippers making offerings to a <a href="/wiki/Stupa" title="Stupa">chedi</a> at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wat_Doi_Suthep" title="Wat Doi Suthep">Wat Doi Suthep</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chiang_Mai" title="Chiang Mai">Chiang Mai</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Thailand\'s prevalent religion is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theravada_Buddhism" title="Theravada Buddhism">Theravada Buddhism</a>, which is an integral part of Thai identity and culture. Active participation in Buddhism is among the highest in the world. According to the 2000 census, 94.6% and 93.58% in 2010 of the country\'s population self-identified as Buddhists of the Theravada tradition. <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Thailand" title="Islam in Thailand">Muslims</a> constitute the second largest religious group in Thailand, comprising 4.29% of the population in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIA_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-US_Department_of_State,_Thailand_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-US_Department_of_State,_Thailand-134">[134]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Islam is concentrated mostly in the country\'s southernmost provinces: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pattani" title="Pattani">Pattani</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yala_Province" title="Yala Province">Yala</a>, <a href="/wiki/Satun_Province" title="Satun Province">Satun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Narathiwat_Province" title="Narathiwat Province">Narathiwat</a>, and part of <a href="/wiki/Songkhla_Province" title="Songkhla Province">Songkhla</a> <a href="/wiki/Chumphon_Province" title="Chumphon Province">Chumphon</a>, which are predominantly <a href="/wiki/Malays_(ethnic_group)" title="Malays (ethnic group)">Malay</a>, most of whom are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunni_Muslim" title="Sunni Muslim">Sunni Muslims</a>. Christians represented 1.17% (2015) of the population in 2015, with the remaining population consisting of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hinduism_in_Thailand#Thailand" title="Hinduism in Thailand">Hindus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sikhism_in_Thailand" title="Sikhism in Thailand">Sikhs</a>, who live mostly in the country\'s cities. There is also a small but historically significant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jews_and_Judaism_in_Thailand" title="Jews and Judaism in Thailand">Jewish community in Thailand</a> dating back to the 17th century.\n</p><p>According to the 2015 census,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto-7">[7]</a></sup> 67,328,562 Thailand residents belonged to the following religious groups:\n</p>\n<table border="1" cellpadding="2" cellspacing="0" class="wikitable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th bgcolor="#efefef">Religion\n</th>\n<th bgcolor="#efefef">Number<br/> (2010)<sup
